The host environment (Claude, Codex, Gemini, Cursor) remains the execution container. Wazir supplies the operating model, guardrails, and artifact contracts.\n     4→\n     5→## Core components\n     6→\n     7→| Component | Purpose |\n     8→|-----------|---------|\n     9→| Roles | Canonical contracts defining what each agent role does and produces |\n    10→| Workflows | Phase entrypoints that sequence roles through delivery |\n    11→| Skills | Reusable procedures (wz:tdd, wz:debugging, wz:verification, wz:brainstorming) |\n    12→| Hooks | Guardrails enforcing protected paths, loop caps, and capture routing |\n    13→| Expertise | 315 curated knowledge modules composed into agent prompts |\n    14→| Templates | Artifact templates for phase outputs and handoff |\n    15→| Schemas | Validation schemas for manifest, hooks, artifacts, and exports |\n    16→| Exports | Generated host packages tailored per supported host |\n    17→| Tooling | CLI for validation, indexing, recall, capture, and status |\n    18→\n    19→## Design influences\n    20→\n    21→| Source | What Wazir keeps |\n    22→|--------|----------------------|\n    23→| Wazir skill system | Disciplined workflows, TDD, verification, review rigor, skill ergonomics |\n    24→| Spec-Kit | Spec-first development, explicit artifacts, approval gates |\n    25→| Oh My Claude | Host-native prompt structure and operator ergonomics |\n    26→| autoresearch | Research loops, experiment tracking, self-improvement discipline |\n    27→\n    28→## Context and indexing\n    29→\n    30→Wazir ships a built-in local index and recall surface that reduces context window consumption by 60-80% on exploration-heavy phases.\n    31→\n    32→### Context tiers\n    33→\n    34→| Tier | Token budget | Content | When to use |\n    35→|------|-------------|---------|-------------|\n    36→| L0 | ~100 tokens | One-line identifier (name, kind, language, line range) | Inventory scans, deciding what to load |\n    37→| L1 | ~500-2k tokens | Structural summary (signature, fields, purpose, dependencies) | Understanding shape without full source |\n    38→| L2 | Full content | Exact line-bounded source slice | Implementation, debugging, code review |\n    39→\n    40→### Role-specific recall defaults\n    41→\n    42→Roles are assigned default tiers based on their information needs:\n    43→\n    44→- **Exploration roles** (clarifier, researcher, specifier, content-author, designer, planner) default to L1 -- they need to understand structure across many files without reading every line\n    45→- **Implementation roles** (executor, verifier) default to direct read (L2) -- they need exact source for code changes and verification\n    46→- **Review role** (reviewer) starts at L1 as a triage pass, escalating to direct read only for logic errors, security concerns, or ambiguous summaries\n    47→- **Learning role** (learner) defaults to L0 -- it needs only inventory-level context to identify what changed\n    48→\n    49→Every role includes a fallback chain: if the preferred tier fails (no index, no summaries), the role falls back to direct file reads. The system degrades gracefully -- no wasted tokens on failed index operations.\n    50→\n    51→### Capture routing\n    52→\n    53→Large tool output is routed to run-local files via `wazir capture route` and `wazir capture output`, keeping the context window lean. The agent receives a file path reference (~50 tokens) instead of the full output.\n    54→\n    55→### Pipeline integration points\n    56→\n    57→The index and recall surface integrates with the pipeline at five points:\n    58→\n    59→1. **Session start** -- the bootstrap hook runs `index refresh` (or `build` for first run) to ensure the index is current\n    60→2. **Scan project** -- the `scan-project` skill runs `index stats` to report index health in the project profile\n    61→3. **Debugging** -- the `wz:debugging` OBSERVE phase uses `index search-symbols` and `recall --tier L1` for symbol-first exploration\n    62→4. **Execute** -- the executor uses `recall file` and `recall symbol` for targeted source reads\n    63→5. **Verify** -- the verifier uses direct reads for full validation\n    64→\n    65→### Measuring savings\n    66→\n    67→`wazir capture usage` generates a per-run token savings report showing capture routing statistics and context window savings. This provides concrete evidence of token reduction per session.\n    68→\n    69→The context-mode integration remains an optional adapter -- off by default, never required for core install, build, or test paths.\n    70→\n    71→See [Indexing and Recall](indexing-and-recall.md) for full command details and extractor documentation.\n    72→\n    73→## Design Phase\n    74→\n    75→The design phase runs after specification and before planning. The `designer` role uses open-pencil MCP tools to produce visual designs from the approved spec. Outputs include a `.fig` design file, exported Tailwind JSX/HTML+CSS scaffolds, design tokens JSON, and screenshot PNGs.\n    76→\n    77→The `design-review` workflow validates designs against the spec before planning begins. The existing `review` workflow also checks design-vs-implementation alignment after execution.\n    78→\n    79→open-pencil is integrated as an optional adapter (`open_pencil`) — it is not required for core Wazir functionality.\n    80→\n    81→## Key references\n    82→\n    83→- [Roles & Workflows](roles-and-workflows.md)\n    84→- [Artifact Model](artifact-model.md)\n    85→- [Host Exports](../reference/host-exports.md)\n    86→- [Hooks](../reference/hooks.md)\n    87→- [Expertise & Antipatterns](composition-engine.md)\n    88→"}]},"uuid":"f07c3708-64f5-4099-a44e-be2ff3db3307","timestamp":"2026-03-20T13:34:03.700Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"27e9e434-da40-4110-8f94-9463c1354ade","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/Users/mohamedabdallah/Work/Wazir","sessionId":"0c5a9bfa-6816-42b1-a97d-37ae7dad26c5","version":"2.1.80","gitBranch":"feat/remaining-6-fixes","slug":"mighty-wondering-giraffe"}

{"parentUuid":"72dc2337-8596-4a94-a5eb-9d16e1563aa3","isSidechain":true,"promptId":"67fcde4a-7483-4526-a2da-9ae9514890c8","agentId":"aab6decea538aac41","type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_01JDxPfkcfMUEii2FxEzwZGX","type":"tool_result","content":"     1→# Roles and Workflows\n     2→\n     3→Wazir defines explicit canonical roles and explicit phase workflows. Understanding why these exist and how they interact is key to using the system effectively.\n     4→\n     5→## Why roles?\n     6→\n     7→Roles are isolation boundaries, not personas. Each role has defined inputs, allowed tools, required outputs, escalation rules, and failure conditions. An agent inside a role cannot write to protected paths, cannot skip required outputs, and must escalate when ambiguity conditions are met. The discipline is structural, not instructional.\n     8→\n     9→Separating concerns into roles means that the entity implementing a task is never the same entity reviewing it. This adversarial separation is what makes quality enforcement structural rather than aspirational.\n    10→\n    11→## Why phases?\n    12→\n    13→Phases are artifact checkpoints, not conversation stages. Every phase consumes a named artifact from the previous phase and produces a named artifact for the next. Nothing flows via conversation history. A session can end, a new agent can pick up the artifacts, and delivery continues — because the handoff is explicit, structured, and schema-validated.\n    14→\n    15→## The phase model\n    16→\n    17→The canonical workflow sequence is:\n    18→\n    19→1. **clarify** — resolve ambiguity before committing to a spec\n    20→2. **discover** — gather research and prior art\n    21→3. **specify** — produce a measurable spec with acceptance criteria\n    22→4. **spec-challenge** — adversarial review of the spec\n    23→5. **author** — finalize content, microcopy, and i18n keys\n    24→6. **design** — produce visual designs from the approved spec\n    25→7. **design-review** — validate designs against spec, accessibility, and visual consistency\n    26→8. **plan** — create an implementation plan from the approved spec and designs\n    27→9. **plan-review** — adversarial review of the plan\n    28→10. **execute** — implement the plan\n    29→11. **verify** — QA hard gate with fresh proof\n    30→12. **review** — adversarial quality review\n    31→13. **learn** — capture scoped learnings\n    32→14. **prepare-next** — produce a clean handoff for the next run\n    33→\n    34→Additionally, **run-audit** is a standalone workflow that can be invoked outside the linear pipeline to perform structured codebase audits with source-backed findings.\n    35→\n    36→## Role routing\n    37→\n    38→The orchestrator dispatches three roles per task: `executor`, `reviewer`, and `verifier`. By default, all three run for every task. The `required_roles` field in a task's YAML frontmatter controls which roles are dispatched, allowing the orchestrator to skip unnecessary roles and save context window budget.\n    39→\n    40→### Auto-detection\n    41→\n    42→The clarifier assigns `required_roles` based on task type:\n    43→\n    44→| Task type | Required roles | Rationale |\n    45→|-----------|---------------|-----------|\n    46→| `feature` | `[executor, reviewer, verifier]` | Full pipeline |\n    47→| `bugfix` | `[executor, reviewer, verifier]` | Full pipeline |\n    48→| `refactor` | `[executor, reviewer, verifier]` | Full pipeline |\n    49→| `docs` | `[executor, verifier]` | No adversarial review needed |\n    50→| `config` | `[executor, verifier]` | No adversarial review needed |\n    51→\n    52→If `security_critical: true`, `reviewer` is always included.\n    53→\n    54→### Constraints\n    55→\n    56→- `verifier` is always present — it is the QA hard gate and cannot be removed\n    57→- Default when `required_roles` is absent: `[executor, reviewer, verifier]` (backward compatible)\n    58→\n    59→## Canonical source\n    60→\n    61→Use the files under `roles/` and `workflows/` as the canonical source for role contracts and phase entrypoints. For exact role and workflow tables, see the [Roles Reference](../reference/roles-reference.md).\n    62→"}]},"uuid":"0898263f-1806-4326-9183-787385ee5fb0","timestamp":"2026-03-20T13:34:03.886Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"72dc2337-8596-4a94-a5eb-9d16e1563aa3","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/Users/mohamedabdallah/Work/Wazir","sessionId":"0c5a9bfa-6816-42b1-a97d-37ae7dad26c5","version":"2.1.80","gitBranch":"feat/remaining-6-fixes","slug":"mighty-wondering-giraffe"}

No questions unless the user explicitly asks for interactive mode.\n     9→\n    10→## Command Routing\n    11→Follow the Canonical Command Matrix in `hooks/routing-matrix.json`.\n    12→- Large commands (test runners, builds, diffs, dependency trees, linting) → context-mode tools\n    13→- Small commands (git status, ls, pwd, wazir CLI) → native Bash\n    14→- If context-mode unavailable, fall back to native Bash with warning\n    15→\n    16→## Codebase Exploration\n    17→1. Inject matching learnings into context, sorted by confidence (highest first), most recent first, limit 10\n    40→   5. Research is best-effort.\n    78→4. The **researcher role** produces the research artifact.\n    79→5. The **reviewer role** runs the research-review loop with `--mode research-review`.\n    80→6. Loop runs for `pass_counts[depth]` passes.\n    81→\n    82→Save result to `.wazir/runs/latest/clarified/research-brief.md`.\n    83→\n    84→**After completing this phase, output to the user:**\n    85→\n    86→> **Research complete.**\n    87→>\n    88→> **Found:** [N] external sources fetched, [N] codebase patterns identified, [N] architectural constraints documented\n    89→>\n    90→> **Without this phase:** Spec would be built on assumptions instead of evidence — wrong framework APIs, missed existing utilities, contradicted naming conventions\n    91→>\n    92→> **Changed because of this work:** [List of key discoveries — e.g., \"found existing auth middleware at src/middleware/auth.ts\", \"project uses Vitest not Jest\"]\n    93→\n    94→### Checkpoint: Research Review\n    95→\n    96→> **Research complete. Every ambiguity left here becomes a 50/50 coin flip during implementation that could produce the wrong feature.\n   118→>\n   119→> **Looking for:** Ambiguous requirements, implicit assumptions, missing constraints, scope boundaries, unresolved questions\n   120→\n   121→### Input Preservation (before producing clarification)\n   122→\n   123→1. Produce:\n   163→\n   164→- **What** we're building — concrete deliverables\n   165→- **Why** — the motivation and business value\n   166→- **Constraints** — technical, timeline, dependencies\n   167→- **Assumptions** — what we're taking as given (each explicitly confirmed by user or clearly stated in input)\n   168→- **Scope boundaries** — what's IN and what's explicitly OUT (every exclusion must reference the user's confirmation: \"Out of scope per user confirmation in question batch 1, Q5\")\n   169→- **Unresolved questions** — anything still ambiguous after question batches\n   170→\n   171→Save to `.wazir/runs/latest/clarified/clarification.md`.\n   172→\n   173→Invoke `wz:reviewer --mode clarification-review`. Route feedback: plan corrections → `user-feedback.md`, new requirements → `briefing.md`.\n   199→\n   200→---\n   201→\n   202→## Sub-Workflow 3: Spec Harden (specify + spec-challenge workflows)\n   203→\n   204→**Before starting this phase, output to the user:**\n   205→\n   206→> **Spec Hardening** — About to convert the clarified scope into a measurable, testable specification and then run adversarial spec-challenge review to find gaps.\n   207→>\n   208→> **Why this matters:** Without hardening, acceptance criteria stay vague (\"it should work well\") instead of measurable (\"response time under 200ms for 95th percentile\"). Vague specs pass any implementation, making review meaningless.\n   209→>\n   210→> **Looking for:** Untestable criteria, missing error handling specs, undefined edge cases, performance requirements, security constraints\n   211→\n   212→Delegate to the specify workflow (`workflows/specify.md`):\n   213→\n   214→1. Auto-enable the `author` workflow if the spec mentions any of:\n   233→- Database seeding, seed data, fixtures, sample records\n   234→- Sample content, placeholder text, demo data\n   235→- Test fixtures, mock API responses, test data files\n   236→- Translations, i18n strings, localization\n   237→- Copy (button labels, error messages, onboarding text)\n   238→- Documentation content, user guides, API docs\n   239→- Email templates, notification text\n   240→\n   241→If detected, set `workflow_policy.author.enabled = true` in the run config and note:\n   242→> **Content needs detected.** The content-author workflow will run after design approval to produce: [list detected content types].\n   243→\n   244→### Checkpoint: Hardened Spec Review\n   245→\n   246→> **Spec hardened. This is where architectural mistakes get caught cheaply instead of discovered during implementation.\n   268→>\n   269→> **Looking for:** Architectural trade-offs, scalability implications, complexity vs. simplicity, alignment with existing codebase patterns\n   270→\n   271→Invoke the `brainstorming` skill (`wz:brainstorming`):\n   272→\n   273→1. The system decides — the user does NOT pick.\n   257→\n   258→| Class | Workflows | Rules |\n   259→|-------|-----------|-------|\n   260→| **Core** (always run) | `clarify`, `execute`, `verify`, `review` | Never skipped |\n   261→| **Adaptive** (run when evidence says so) | `discover`, `design`, `author`, `specify` | Skipped for bugfix/docs/spike at quick depth |\n   262→| **Scale** (intensity varies) | `spec-challenge`, `plan-review`, `design-review` | Loop cap controls iteration depth |\n   263→| **Post-run** (always run) | `learn`, `prepare_next` | Part of Final Review phase |\n   264→\n   265→Log skip decisions with reasons in `workflow_policy`.\n   266→\n   267→### Confidence Gate\n   268→\n   269→After building run config:\n   270→\n   271→- **High confidence** — one-line summary and proceed:\n   272→  > **Running: standard depth, feature, sequential. Proceeding...**\n   273→\n   274→- **Low confidence** — show plan and ask:\n   275→\n   276→  Ask the user via AskUserQuestion:\n   277→  - **Question:** \"Does this run configuration look right?\"\n   278→  - **Options:**\n   279→    1. \"Yes, proceed\" *(Recommended)*\n   280→    2. \"No, let me adjust\"\n   281→\n   282→  Wait for the user's selection before continuing.\n   283→\n   284→```bash\n   285→wazir capture event --run <run-id> --event phase_exit --phase init --status completed\n   286→```\n   287→\n   288→Run the phase report and display it to the user:\n   289→```bash\n   290→wazir report phase --run <run-id> --phase init\n   291→```\n   292→\n   293→Output the report content to the user in the conversation.\n   294→\n   295→---\n   296→\n   297→# Interaction Modes\n   298→\n   299→The `interaction_mode` field in run-config controls how the pipeline interacts with the user:\n   300→\n   301→| Mode | Inline modifier | Behavior | Best for |\n   302→|------|----------------|----------|----------|\n   303→| **`guided`** | (default) | Pipeline runs, pauses at phase checkpoints for user approval. Current default behavior. | Most work |\n   304→| **`auto`** | `/wazir auto ...` | No human checkpoints. Codex reviews all. Gating agent decides continue/loop_back/escalate. Stops ONLY on escalate. | Overnight, clear spec, well-understood domain |\n   305→| **`interactive`** | `/wazir interactive ...` | More questions, more discussion, co-designs with user. Researcher presents options. Executor checks approach before coding. | Ambiguous requirements, new domain, learning |\n   306→\n   307→## `auto` mode constraints\n   308→\n   309→- **Codex REQUIRED** — refuse to start auto mode if `multi_tool.codex` is not configured in `.wazir/state/config.json`. Error: \"Auto mode requires an external reviewer (Codex). Configure it first or use guided mode.\"\n   310→- **On escalate:** STOP immediately, write the escalation reason to `.wazir/runs/<id>/escalations/`, and wait for user input\n   311→- **Wall-clock limit:** default 4 hours. If exceeded, stop with escalation.\n   312→- **Never auto-commits to main** — always work on feature branch\n   313→- All checkpoints (AskUserQuestion) are skipped — gating agent evaluates phase reports and decides\n   314→\n   315→## `guided` mode (default)\n   316→\n   317→Current behavior — no changes needed. Checkpoints at phase boundaries, user approves before advancing.\n   318→\n   319→## `interactive` mode\n   320→\n   321→- **Clarifier:** asks more detailed questions, presents research findings with options: \"I found 3 approaches — which interests you?\"\n   322→- **Executor:** checks approach before coding: \"I'm about to implement auth with Supabase — sound right?\"\n   323→- **Reviewer:** discusses findings with user, not just presents verdict: \"I found a potential auth bypass — here's why I think it's high severity, do you agree?\"\n   324→- Slower but highest quality for complex/ambiguous work\n   325→\n   326→## Mode checking in phase skills\n   327→\n   328→All phase skills check `interaction_mode` from run-config at every checkpoint:\n   329→\n   330→```\n   331→# Read from run-config\n   332→interaction_mode = run_config.interaction_mode ?? 'guided'\n   333→\n   334→# At each checkpoint:\n   335→if interaction_mode == 'auto':\n   336→    # Skip checkpoint, let gating agent decide\n   337→elif interaction_mode == 'interactive':\n   338→    # More detailed question, present options, discuss\n   339→else:\n   340→    # guided — standard checkpoint with AskUserQuestion\n   341→```\n   342→\n   343→---\n   344→\n   345→# Two-Level Phase Model\n   346→\n   347→The pipeline has 4 top-level **phases**, each containing multiple **workflows** with review loops:\n   348→\n   349→```\n   350→Phase 1: Init\n   351→  └── (inline — no sub-workflows)\n   352→\n   353→Phase 2: Clarifier\n   354→  ├── discover (research) ← research-review loop\n   355→  ├── clarify ← clarification-review loop\n   356→  ├── specify ← spec-challenge loop\n   357→  ├── author (adaptive) ← approval gate\n   358→  ├── design ← design-review loop\n   359→  └── plan ← plan-review loop\n   360→\n   361→Phase 3: Executor\n   362→  ├── execute (per-task) ← task-review loop per task\n   363→  └── verify\n   364→\n   365→Phase 4: Final Review\n   366→  ├── review (final) ← scored review\n   367→  ├── learn\n   368→  └── prepare_next\n   369→```\n   370→\n   371→**Event capture uses both levels.** When emitting phase events, include `--parent-phase`:\n   372→```bash\n   373→wazir capture event --run <id> --event phase_enter --phase discover --parent-phase clarifier --status in_progress\n   374→```\n   375→\n   376→**Progress markers between workflows:** After each workflow completes, output:\n   377→> Phase 2: Clarifier > Workflow: specify (3 of 6 workflows complete)\n   378→\n   379→**`wazir status` shows both levels:** \"Phase 2: Clarifier > Workflow: specify\"\n   380→\n   381→---\n   382→\n   383→# Phase 2: Clarifier\n   384→\n   385→**Before starting this phase, output to the user:**\n   386→\n   387→> **Clarifier Phase** — About to research your codebase, clarify requirements, harden the spec, brainstorm designs, and produce an execution plan.\n   388→>\n   389→> **Why this matters:** Without this, I'd guess your tech stack, misunderstand constraints, miss edge cases in the spec, and build the wrong architecture. Every ambiguity left unresolved here becomes a bug or rework cycle later.\n   390→>\n   391→> **Looking for:** Unstated assumptions, scope boundaries, conflicting requirements, missing acceptance criteria\n   392→\n   393→```bash\n   394→wazir capture event --run <run-id> --event phase_enter --phase clarifier --status in_progress\n   395→```\n   396→\n   397→Invoke the `wz:clarifier` skill. It handles all sub-workflows internally:\n   398→\n   399→1. **Source Capture** — fetch URLs from input\n   400→2. **Research** (discover workflow) — codebase + external research\n   401→3. **Clarify** (clarify workflow) — scope, constraints, assumptions\n   402→4. **Spec Harden** (specify + spec-challenge workflows) — measurable spec\n   403→5. **Brainstorm** (design + design-review workflows) — design approaches\n   404→6. **Plan** (plan + plan-review workflows) — execution plan\n   405→\n   406→Each sub-workflow has its own review loop. User checkpoints between major steps.\n   407→\n   408→### Scope Invariant\n   409→\n   410→**Hard rule:** `items_in_plan >= items_in_input` unless the user explicitly approves scope reduction. The clarifier MUST NOT autonomously tier, defer, or drop items from the user's input. It can suggest prioritization, but the decision belongs to the user.\n   411→\n   412→Output: approved spec + design + execution plan in `.wazir/runs/latest/clarified/`.\n   413→\n   414→**After completing this phase, output to the user:**\n   415→\n   416→> **Clarifier Phase complete.**\n   417→>\n   418→> **Found:** [N] ambiguities resolved, [N] assumptions made explicit, [N] scope boundaries drawn, [N] acceptance criteria hardened\n   419→>\n   420→> **Without this phase:** Requirements would be interpreted differently across tasks, acceptance criteria would be vague and untestable, the design would be ad-hoc, and the plan would miss dependency ordering\n   421→>\n   422→> **Changed because of this work:** [List spec tightening changes, resolved questions, design decisions, scope adjustments]\n   423→\n   424→```bash\n   425→wazir capture event --run <run-id> --event phase_exit --phase clarifier --status completed\n   426→```\n   427→\n   428→Run the phase report and display savings to the user:\n   429→```bash\n   430→wazir report phase --run <run-id> --phase clarifier\n   431→wazir stats --run <run-id>\n   432→```\n   433→\n   434→**Show savings in conversation output:**\n   435→> **Context savings this phase:** Used wazir index for [N] queries and context-mode for [M] commands, saving ~[X] tokens ([Y]% reduction). This ensures the learn workflow always produces output when enabled.\n   561→\n   562→### 4c: Prepare Next (planner role)\n   563→\n   564→Prepare context and handoff for the next run:\n   565→- Write handoff document\n   566→- Compress/archive unneeded files\n   567→- Record what's left to do\n   568→\n   569→**After completing this phase, output to the user:**\n   570→\n   571→> **Final Review Phase complete.**\n   572→>\n   573→> **Found:** [N] findings across 7 dimensions, [N] blocking issues, [N] warnings, [N] learnings proposed for future runs\n   574→>\n   575→> **Without this phase:** Implementation drift from the original request would ship undetected, untested paths would hide production bugs, and recurring mistakes would never get captured as learnings\n   576→>\n   577→> **Changed because of this work:** [List of findings fixed, score achieved, learnings extracted, handoff prepared]\n   578→\n   579→```bash\n   580→wazir capture event --run <run-id> --event phase_exit --phase final_review --status completed\n   581→```\n   582→\n   583→Run the phase report and display it to the user:\n   584→```bash\n   585→wazir report phase --run <run-id> --phase final_review\n   586→```\n   587→\n   588→Output the report content to the user in the conversation.\n   589→\n   590→---\n   591→\n   592→## Step 5: CHANGELOG + Gitflow Validation (Hard Gates)\n   593→\n   594→Before presenting results:\n   595→\n   596→```bash\n   597→wazir validate changelog --require-entries --base main\n   598→wazir validate commits --base main\n   599→```\n   600→\n   601→Both must pass before PR. Restructure into 4 clear phases:\n    10→\n    11→### Phase 1: Init\n    12→- Straightforward setup\n    13→- Read config, check prerequisites, create run directory\n    14→- No questions (depth/intent/teams inferred or defaulted — see items #4, #5)\n    15→- Scan `input/` directory automatically (see item #3)\n    16→\n    17→### Phase 2: Clarifier\n    18→- Everything from reading input to having an implementation plan\n    19→- Includes: research, clarification, spec hardening, brainstorming/design, planning\n    20→- All review loops happen within this phase (research-review, clarification-review, spec-challenge, design-review, plan-review)\n    21→- Output: approved spec + design + execution plan with task specs\n    22→\n    23→### Phase 3: Executor\n    24→- The simplest phase — pure implementation\n    25→- Should work with a lower-quality model (e.g., Sonnet for executor, medium-quality for per-task reviewer)\n    26→- Per-task TDD cycle: write test → implement → review against task spec\n    27→- The per-task reviewer checks implementation against the TASK spec (not input)\n    28→- Should still produce the same quality results with cheaper models\n    29→\n    30→### Phase 4: Final Review\n    31→- Compare implementation against the ORIGINAL INPUT (not the task specs)\n    32→- The executor's per-task reviewer already validated against task specs — that's covered\n    33→- Final reviewer validates: does what we built actually match what the user asked for?\n    34→- Input-to-implementation comparison catches drift that task-level review misses\n    35→- After review: apply learnings (from the run)\n    36→- After review: prepare for next task (compress/archive unneeded files, write handoff)\n    37→- This is where learn + prepare-next phases get folded in\n    38→\n    39→### Key Principle\n    40→- Phase 3 executor reviewer reviews: implementation vs. task spec\n    41→- Phase 4 final reviewer reviews: implementation vs. original user input\n    42→- These are different concerns — task-level correctness vs. intent alignment\n    43→\n    44→### Online Research\n    45→- How MetaGPT structures its pipeline phases (ProductManager → Architect → Engineer → QA) — their phase boundaries and handoff contracts\n    46→- How OpenAI Symphony structures Poll → Dispatch → Resolve → Land — what happens between phases\n    47→- How CrewAI Flows handle phase transitions with routers and conditional branching\n    48→- How GitHub Spec-Kit structures spec → implement → verify — their phase model\n    49→- LangGraph's multi-step agent pipelines — how they define phase boundaries vs. node boundaries\n    50→- How Devin structures its planning → implementation → verification phases\n    51→- How Cursor Agent / Windsurf Cascade structure their internal pipeline phases\n    52→\n    53→---\n    54→\n    55→## 16. One-Command Install + Zero-Config Start\n    56→\n    57→**Goal:** Wazir should be one of the easiest agent frameworks to install and use.\n    58→\n    59→**Current experience (too many steps, one breaks):**\n    60→```bash\n    61→git clone wazir\n    62→npm install\n    63→npx wazir export build   # 4 host exports\n    64→npx wazir init            # interactive arrows — FAILS in Claude Code\n    65→# then learn phases, roles, skills, composition...\n    66→```\n    67→\n    68→**Target experience:**\n    69→```bash\n    70→npx wazir\n    71→```\n    72→\n    73→One command. Everything else is automatic or deferred until needed.\n    74→\n    75→**What `npx wazir` should do:**\n    76→1. Detect host (Claude Code? Codex? Cursor? Gemini?) from environment\n    77→2. Export the right files for that host automatically\n    78→3. Scan the project (language, framework, stack)\n    79→4. Ask \"What do you want to build?\" — one question, no config\n    80→5. Run the full pipeline\n    81→\n    82→**No init questions.** Defaults for everything:\n    83→- Depth: standard (override with `/wazir deep ...`)\n    84→- Intent: inferred from request text\n    85→- Teams: sequential (always)\n    86→- Model mode: detected from available tools\n    87→\n    88→**Install paths (from easiest to most control):**\n    89→\n    90→| Path | Command | Who |\n    91→|------|---------|-----|\n    92→| Plugin marketplace | `/plugin install wazir` | Claude Code users |\n    93→| npx (zero install) | `npx @wazir-dev/cli` | Any Node project |\n    94→| Global install | `npm i -g @wazir-dev/cli` | Power users |\n    95→| Clone + link | `git clone && npm link` | Contributors |\n    96→\n    97→**First-run experience:**\n    98→- No CLAUDE.md editing required\n    99→- No config files to create\n   100→- No concepts to learn upfront\n   101→- Just: install → `/wazir build me a login page` → pipeline runs\n   102→\n   103→**Deep when you need it:**\n   104→- `wazir config` for power users who want control\n   105→- `wazir doctor` to see what's configured\n   106→- `wazir stats` to see what Wazir saved you\n   107→- Expertise modules, review loops, composition — all there, all optional to understand\n   108→\n   109→**Principle:** Like git — `git init` is one command, `git config` is deep. Instant start, deep when you need it.\n   110→\n   111→### Online Research\n   112→- How superpowers plugin handles install (`/plugin marketplace add`) — onboarding flow, zero-config\n   113→- How Cursor rules / .cursorrules get auto-detected and applied — zero-config pattern\n   114→- How `create-react-app`, `create-next-app`, `create-t3-app` handle project scaffolding — one-command patterns\n   115→- How Vercel CLI (`npx vercel`) auto-detects framework and deploys — zero-config detection\n   116→- How `degit` (Rich Harris) handles instant project scaffolding without git history\n   117→- How Yeoman generators and `npm create` handle interactive scaffolding vs. zero-config\n   118→- How the Claude Code plugin marketplace install flow works (behind the scenes)\n   119→- How OpenAI Codex handles first-run experience — what config does it need?\n   120→- How aider (Paul Gauthier) handles zero-config start — `aider` just works in any repo\n   121→- How bolt.new / v0.dev handle instant project start with no config\n   122→\n   123→---\n   124→\n   125→## 15. SQLite State Database for Learnings, Findings, and Trends\n   126→\n   127→**What:** Extend the existing SQLite infrastructure (already used for index) with a `state.sqlite` for persistent cross-run data.\n   128→\n   129→**Why files don't work:** Can't query \"show me all executor learnings for node stack\", can't detect recurring findings, can't trend across runs without reading every past report.\n   130→\n   131→**New tables in `state.sqlite` (separate from `index.sqlite` to survive rebuilds):**\n   132→- `learnings` — id, source_run, category, scope_roles, scope_stacks, scope_concerns, confidence, recurrence_count, content, created_at, last_applied, expires_at\n   133→- `findings` — id, run_id, phase, source (internal/codex/self-audit), severity, description, resolved, finding_hash (for dedup/recurrence detection)\n   134→- `audit_history` — run_id, date, finding_count, fix_count, manual_count, quality_score_before, quality_score_after\n   135→- `usage_aggregate` — run_id, date, tokens_saved, bytes_avoided, savings_ratio, index_queries, routing_decisions\n   136→\n   137→**Key queries this enables:**\n   138→- `SELECT * FROM findings WHERE finding_hash IN (SELECT finding_hash FROM findings GROUP BY finding_hash HAVING COUNT(*) >= 2)` — recurring findings → auto-propose as learnings\n   139→- `SELECT run_id, finding_count FROM audit_history ORDER BY date` — trend tracking\n   140→- `SELECT * FROM learnings WHERE scope_stacks LIKE '%node%' AND confidence = 'high'` — targeted learning injection\n   141→- `SELECT SUM(tokens_saved) FROM usage_aggregate` — cumulative savings\n   142→\n   143→**No new dependency:** `node:sqlite` already used by the index. Same infrastructure, new file.\n   144→\n   145→### Online Research\n   146→- How CrewAI stores long-term memory (SQLite3 backend) — schema, query patterns, adaptive scoring\n   147→- How LangGraph Store uses PostgreSQL + pgvector for cross-session persistence — schema design\n   148→- How LangMem stores and retrieves memories with TTL and semantic search\n   149→- How Mem0 (formerly EmbedChain) structures its memory database — entities, relations, facts\n   150→- How OpenHands stores agent state and history across sessions\n   151→- SQLite FTS5 full-text search for finding content — already used by context-mode plugin\n   152→- How Datasette (Simon Willison) exposes SQLite as a browsable interface — could be useful for `wazir stats`\n   153→- How Litestream handles SQLite replication — relevant if state.sqlite needs backup/sync\n   154→- SQLite WAL mode for concurrent reads during pipeline execution\n   155→\n   156→---\n   157→\n   158→## 14. Self-Audit V2 — Learnings + Trend Tracking + Severity\n   159→\n   160→**What:** Current self-audit finds and fixes issues but doesn't learn from them. Needs a second pass.\n   161→\n   162→**What's missing:**\n   163→\n   164→1. **No learning** — findings should feed into `memory/learnings/proposed/`. If the same issue recurs across audits, it becomes an accepted learning injected into executor context to prevent the issue from being created.\n   165→\n   166→2. **No comparison to previous audits** — each audit starts fresh with no awareness of what was found last time. Should track: \"last audit found 12 issues, this time 8 — are they the same or new?\" Trend detection across runs.\n   167→\n   168→3. **Fixes are mechanical only** — can fix lint, stale exports, broken references. Can't fix architectural drift, skill degradation, logic issues. These become \"manual required\" that nobody acts on. Need: escalation path for manual findings (create a task? file an issue?).\n   169→\n   170→4. **No severity or prioritization** — all findings are equal. Should have severity levels: critical (abort loop), high (fix now), medium (fix if time), low (log and skip).\n   171→\n   172→5. **Doesn't audit its own effectiveness** — after 5 loops, did the fixes improve anything? Need a before/after quality score per loop to measure convergence quality, not just convergence count.\n   173→\n   174→**Connection to item #13:** Self-audit findings should feed the SAME learning pipeline as review findings. Both are signals about recurring quality issues.\n   175→\n   176→### Online Research\n   177→- How SonarQube tracks code quality trends across builds — severity levels, technical debt measurement\n   178→- How CodeClimate maintains quality scores over time — GPA model, trend detection\n   179→- How GitHub Advanced Security tracks recurring vulnerabilities — alert lifecycle (open → dismissed → fixed → reopened)\n   180→- How ESLint's `--cache` tracks which files have been linted before — incremental audit pattern\n   181→- How Snyk tracks dependency vulnerabilities across versions — recurring vs. new findings\n   182→- How PagerDuty/OpsGenie handle alert fatigue and dedup — relevant for finding dedup\n   183→- How Netflix Chaos Monkey evolved its audit approach — game days, severity escalation\n   184→- Academic: \"Technical Debt Detection and Management\" survey papers — automated detection patterns\n   185→\n   186→---\n   187→\n   188→## 13. Internal Review First, Codex Second + Review Findings Feed Learning\n   189→\n   190→**What:** Restructure the review loop: internal review skill first (fast, cheap, expertise-loaded), Codex second (slow, expensive, fresh eyes). Review findings must feed the learning system.\n   191→\n   192→**Current problem:**\n   193→- Everything goes to Codex first — slow, expensive, overkill for most findings\n   194→- Review findings are consumed and forgotten — same mistakes repeat across runs\n   195→- Wazir is not evolving from its own reviews\n   196→\n   197→**New review flow:**\n   198→1. **Internal review** — `wz:reviewer` with full expertise modules composed in context (composition engine). Catches ~80% of issues. Uses Sonnet (not Opus). Fast and cheap.\n   199→2. **Fix cycle** — executor fixes internal findings, re-review internally until clean\n   200→3. **Codex review** — only AFTER internal review passes. Fresh eyes on clean code. Catches subtle issues the internal reviewer missed.\n   201→4. **Fix cycle** — executor fixes Codex findings\n   202→5. **Learning extraction** — ALL review findings (internal + Codex) feed into the learning system\n   203→\n   204→**Review findings → Learning pipeline:**\n   205→- After each run, the learner role scans all review findings\n   206→- Findings that recur across 2+ runs → auto-proposed as learnings\n   207→- Recurring learnings get injected into future executor context\n   208→- Example: if Codex keeps finding \"missing error handling in async functions\" across 3 runs, that becomes an accepted learning injected into every future executor prompt\n   209→- This is how Wazir evolves — it stops making the same mistakes\n   210→\n   211→**Reference skills:**\n   212→- superpowers: `requesting-code-review` (dispatches review subagent)\n   213→- CodeRabbit: `code-reviewer` (autonomous review agent)\n   214→- Wazir: `wz:reviewer` (phase-aware, multi-mode)\n   215→\n   216→**Decision needed:** Should internal review use a dedicated review subagent (like superpowers) or the current inline reviewer skill?\n   217→\n   218→### Online Research\n   219→- How superpowers `requesting-code-review` dispatches review subagents — architecture and prompt design\n   220→- How CodeRabbit AI review agent works — what it checks, how it structures findings\n   221→- How GitHub Copilot code review works — inline suggestions vs. PR-level review\n   222→- How Amazon CodeGuru Reviewer detects recurring patterns — ML-based finding detection\n   223→- How Google's Critique (internal code review tool) prioritizes findings\n   224→- How Facebook/Meta's Sapienz handles automated test generation from review findings\n   225→- How Sourcery.ai structures automated code review — rules vs. AI vs. hybrid\n   226→- How Qodo (formerly CodiumAI) generates review-driven test suggestions\n   227→- LangMem's prompt optimization from feedback — how review findings become prompt improvements\n   228→- Academic: \"Learning from Code Reviews\" — mining patterns from historical review data\n   229→\n   230→---\n   231→\n   232→## 12. Opus Orchestrator with Model-Aware Delegation\n   233→\n   234→**What:** Opus acts as orchestrator and decides which model handles each sub-task. Not \"Phase 3 uses Sonnet\" — every sub-task across all phases gets the cheapest model that can do it.\n   235→\n   236→**Examples:**\n   237→| Sub-task | Model | Why |\n   238→|----------|-------|-----|\n   239→| Orchestration / decisions | Opus | Needs judgment |\n   240→| Fetch URL content | Haiku | Mechanical, no reasoning |\n   241→| Read/summarize a file | Sonnet | Comprehension, not deep reasoning |\n   242→| Write implementation code | Sonnet | Good spec + plan = mechanical |\n   243→| Per-task code review | Sonnet | Diff review against clear spec |\n   244→| Spec hardening / design | Opus | Creativity + adversarial thinking |\n   245→| Final review (input vs impl) | Opus | Holistic judgment |\n   246→| Apply learnings | Sonnet | Structured extraction |\n   247→| Write handoff / compress | Haiku | Mechanical file operations |\n   248→\n   249→**How:** Claude Code supports `model` field in agent frontmatter (`model: sonnet | opus | haiku | inherit`). Opus spawns subagents with explicit model overrides via the Agent tool.\n   250→\n   251→**What needs to change:**\n   252→- Implement the `multi-model` mode in config (exists as option, never built)\n   253→- Add model-routing logic: Opus decides model per sub-task based on task complexity\n   254→- Composition engine dispatch includes model selection\n   255→- Skills/workflows annotate which sub-tasks are Haiku/Sonnet/Opus-grade\n   256→\n   257→**Principle:** Not all clarifier sub-agents should be Opus. The agent that grabs data from the internet doesn't need Opus. The one that designs the architecture does.\n   258→\n   259→### Online Research\n   260→- How BAMAS (Budget-Aware Multi-Agent Structuring) models cost budgets in multi-agent systems — academic paper\n   261→- How AutoGen/AG2 handles model routing across agent roles — model selection per agent\n   262→- How CrewAI assigns different LLMs to different agents — per-agent model configuration\n   263→- How LangGraph routes to different models based on task complexity — conditional model selection\n   264→- How OpenRouter handles model routing and fallback — API-level model selection patterns\n   265→- How Anthropic's own Claude Agent SDK handles model selection for subagents\n   266→- How Martian's model router selects optimal model per query — automated model routing\n   267→- Token cost comparison across models (Opus vs Sonnet vs Haiku) for different task types\n   268→- How the Mixture of Agents (MoA) pattern uses cheap models for drafting, expensive for refinement\n   269→\n   270→---\n   271→\n   272→## 11. Interrupts allow you to pause graph execution at specific points and wait for external input before continuing, enabling human-in-the-loop patterns where you need external input to proceed.\n\n**Resume Mechanism:**\nWhen you're ready to continue, you resume execution by re-invoking the graph using Command, which then becomes the return value of the interrupt() call from inside the node. When resuming with Command(resume=value), the value is applied to the next unresolved interrupt in sequence.\n\n**Multiple Interrupts:**\nMultiple interrupt() calls in the same node receive resume values in order. Additionally, you can resume multiple interrupts at once using a dictionary: Command(resume={\"interrupt_id_1\": \"value1\", \"interrupt_id_2\": \"value2\"}).\n\n**Human-in-the-Loop Applications:**\nThe function accepts any JSON-serializable value which is surfaced to the caller. This cascade approach is central to how the system works.\n\n## Specification and Plan Flow\n\nThe workflow has two key editing points:\n\n**Specification Phase**: When you prompt Copilot Workspace to make a change, it starts by reading your codebase and generating a specification. The spec is comprised of two bullet-point lists: one for the current state of the codebase, and one for the desired state. You can edit both lists, either to correct the system's understanding of the current codebase, or to refine the requirements for the desired state.\n\n**Plan Phase**: Once you're happy with the spec, Copilot Workspace will generate a concrete plan for executing the changes. In this step, it will list out every file it intends to create, modify, or delete, as well as a bullet-point list of the actions to be taken in each file. Once again, you can edit any part of this plan — altering the specific instructions, or even changing the list of files that will be affected.\n\n## Downstream Cascade Regeneration\n\nWhen you perform a natural language revision, the plan is now updated incrementally, as opposed to being completely regenerated. When you call interrupt within a node, LangGraph saves the current graph state and waits for you to resume execution with input.\n\nYou resume execution by re-invoking the graph using Command, which then becomes the return value of the interrupt() call from inside the node.\n\n## State Management with Interrupts\n\nInterrupts allow you to pause graph execution and wait for human input, while commands enable dynamic control over the graph's flow. Checkpointing is crucial because it persists the state during these pauses.\n\n## Known Challenges in 2025\n\nSeveral issues have been reported in recent months:\n\n- Users have raised concerns about using `update_state` in LangGraph without potentially blowing away state like human-in-the-loop interrupts. {"parentUuid":"7fc113f9-b746-4420-80ba-3cfd9b259695","isSidechain":true,"promptId":"993bfee3-85a0-4b7f-b82a-ba53f3d6daa1","agentId":"aab6decea538aac41","type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_01FmNnnRVayaouixuzgf1zuu","type":"tool_result","content":"# Copilot Workspace: Overview\n\n[Copilot Workspace](https://githubnext.com/projects/copilot-workspace/) is a _task-centric_ AI assistant. Each day as a developer you start with a task, and make the journey to explore, understand, refine, and complete that task, a journey that can be exciting, challenging, fascinating, and rewarding. Copilot Workspace takes this journey with you, every step of the way — the journey from task to working code. \n\nCopilot Workspace is built on a set of principles that guide its design and development:\n\n* Copilot Workspace is _contextual_. It is deeply integrated with GitHub, and is aware of the context of your task — the repository, the issue, the pull request.\n\n* Copilot Workspace is _assistive_. It offers a canvas for you to navigate unfamiliar tasks, augmenting your development skills with a new kind of AI assistance.\n\n* Copilot Workspace is _pervasive_. It is ready and waiting for you, available on every issue in every enabled repository on GitHub. And Copilot Workspace is even there for you when starting new code, available on every template repository, to create new software using natural language.\n\n* Copilot Workspace is _iterative_. Copilot Workspace encourages you to check, review, refine and iterate on AI-generated outputs. You, the developer, are in control.\n\n* Copilot Workspace is _collaborative_. You can share sessions with your team and publish links to your sessions on issues and pull requests. And, if you're a repository maintainer, we give you controls to help manage the use of AI-assisted development with your repositories.\n\n* Copilot Workspace is _configurable_. You can integrate Copilot Workspace into your workflows via deep links to Copilot Workspace that specify common tasks.\n\nIn this manual, we will guide you through the concepts and features of Copilot Workspace, and help you get started with using it effectively.\n\n<img src=\"images/overview/session.png\" width=\"600\" alt=\"A fully-implemented workspace session\">\n\n*A fully-implemented workspace session*\n\n## Features\n\n1. [Task](#task)\n1. [Specification](#specification)\n1. [Plan](#plan)\n1. [Implementation](#implementation)\n1. [Iterating on Files](#iterating-on-files)\n1. [Integrated Terminal](#integrated-terminal)\n1. [Session Sharing](#session-sharing)\n1. [Task Completion](#task-completion)\n1. [Session Dashboard](#session-dashboard)\n\n## Task\n\nEverything in Copilot Workspace begins with a “task”, which is a natural language description of intent. The task always has a context: a GitHub repository.\nFor this technical preview, Copilot Workspace supports four types of tasks: solving issues, refining pull requests, [creating repositories from templates](creating-repos.md) and ad-hoc tasks. Here we focus on solving issues, which are the most common entry point.\n\nOnce enrolled in the technical preview, then on every issue in GitHub you will find a new \"Open in Workspace\" button:\n\n<img src=\"images/general/open-in-workspace.png\" width=400 alt=\"Button on issue page to open in Copilot Workspace\">\n\n*Open an issue in Copilot Workspace*\n\nThis will open Copilot Workspace contextualized to this issue. For issue tasks, the task is based in the title and body of the issue, plus the issue’s comment thread. Copilot Workspace will immediately progress to the next step in the timeline. This looks like this:\n\n<img src=\"images/overview/issue-timeline-representation.png\" width=600 alt=\"Issue task timeline representation\">\n\n*The task is labeled as “Issue” and analysis begins*\n\n## Specification\n\nIn order to help summarize a non-trivial task definition (e.g. an issue with a long comment thread), Copilot Workspace first generates a “topic” for the task, which takes the form of a question that can be posed against the codebase, and used to define the before/after success criteria (see the [specification](#specification) section below). \n\n<img src=\"images/overview/topic-question.png\" width=600 alt=\"Topic question\">\n\n*Note how the topic introduces clarity that is completely missing from the issue title*\n\nYou can think of the topic as a way to distill the task down to its essence, and to give you an early and fast opportunity to see if Copilot Workspace is on the right track. If the topic is wrong, you don't need to continue. But if the topic is right, it helps you better understand the task, and to focus on the most important aspects of the codebase that are relevant to the task.\n\nAfter producing the topic, Copilot Workspace generates a bulleted list describing the current behavior of the codebase, based on the task and topic being posed. This helps build your confidence that Copilot Workspace is on the right track, and serves as a means of onboarding you to the context, in cases where you might not fully understand the current state.\n\n<img src=\"images/overview/current-spec.png\" width=600 alt=\"Current specification\">\n\n*The current specification answers the question in the topic based on the current state*\n\nAnd if Copilot Workspace gets anything wrong, then you can easily edit/delete steps from the current spec, or even choose to regenerate an entirely new spec (“try again”). In practice, we find that these tend to be pretty good on the first try.\n\nAfter the current specification, Copilot Workspace generates a “proposed specification”, which is a bulleted list which articulates the state that the codebase would be in after resolving the task (effectively answering the question in the topic). And in particular, the proposed specification is focused on defining the success criteria of the task, as opposed to getting into implementation details (which is the role of the [plan](#plan)).\n\n<img src=\"images/overview/proposed-spec.png\" width=600 alt=\"Proposed specification\">\n\n*The proposed specification indicates how to edit the codebase in order to solve the task*\n\n## Content Selection\n\nTo generate the current and proposed specifications, and for all following steps, Copilot Workspace needs to identify which files in the codebase are relevant to understanding and completing the task. It does this by a combination of LLM techniques and traditional code search. The contents of the highest-ranked files are then used as context for nearly all steps in the workflow.\n\nUsers may review the files selected by Copilot Workspace using the \"View references\" button in the Specification panel. To adjust which files are selected, users can edit the task and use natural language to specify which files are relevant.\n\n<img src=\"images/overview/references.png\" width=600 alt=\"Show references dialog\">\n\n*The references that the model used to generate the original and modified specifications*\n\n## Plan\n\nOnce you are happy with the current and proposed specs, you can request Copilot Workspace to generate a plan, which is a list of the files that need to be modified (e.g. edited, created, deleted, moved, or renamed) in order to accomplish the success criteria of the proposed spec. Additionally, each changed file includes a list of specific steps that indicate the exact changes that need to be made.\n\nLike the spec, the plan is fully editable and regeneratable, which allows you to refine and steer Copilot Workspace in the right direction.\n\n<img src=\"images/overview/plan.png\" width=600 alt=\"Plan\">\n\n*A plan, showing the steps needed to edit one file and add a second one*\n\n## Implementation\n\nWhen you are happy with the plan, you can click the “Implement” button in order to begin implementing it. This will update the UI to display a series of queued file updates on the right side, and then begin generating the updated file contents one-by-one. When a file begins generating, its associated entry in the plan will show it as being in progress. And when it completes, the plan will indicate it as being done.\n\nOnce a file is implemented, Copilot Workspace renders a diff view for it, and automatically scrolls to the first change. The diff editors are editable, which allows making minor tweaks directly to the code, as opposed to iterating via changes to the task, spec, or plan.\n\n## Iterating on Files\n\nCopilot Workspace doesn't always get everything right, and so it makes it easy for users to iterate on the implementations file by file. Simply add, remove, edit the items in the plan steps for each file, select the checkbox, and click the \"Update selected files\" button. This will re-generate the contents of the selected files and update the diff view.\n\nFor example, you can edit the diff directly, or you can go back to the plan and make changes there. And if you need to make more extensive changes, you can regenerate the plan entirely.\n\n<img src=\"images/overview/file-iteration.png\" width=600 alt=\"Plan panel with file iteration\">\n\n*The plan panel enables users to iterate on implementation file by file*\n\n## Integrated Terminal\n\nOnce you have implemented the plan, Copilot Workspace enables you to validate the changes for correctness by bringing up an integrated terminal and executing shell commands. This allows performing a build, lint, test, etc. against the changes, and can be a quick and effective way to gain confidence about the task and its completion status. The terminal is backed by a Codespace, so it is a secure sandbox with a full development environment installed. \n\n<img src=\"images/overview/terminal.png\" width=600 alt=\"Integrated terminal\">\n\n*Integrated terminal, showing the generated branch name and access to just-in-time compute*\n\nIf you want to make any more extensive changes or leverage rich editor features (e.g. step debugging), you can open the Copilot Workspace session in a Codespace, using any of Codespace’s supported clients.\n\n## Session Sharing\n\nIn order to make it easy to share a workspace session with others (e.g. for doing an ad-hoc code review or sharing an initial implementation idea), Copilot Workspace allows users to generate shared links. These links can be shared with guests, even if they are not part of the Copilot Workspace preview.\n\nShared sessions are copies of the original session. Non-guest users can use them as a starting point to continue the task or explore alternative solutions without interfering with the original session. Guest users can view the session but cannot use the workspace to make changes.\n\n<img src=\"images/overview/share-link.png\" width=600 alt=\"Generating a share link\">\n\n*Generating a share link from the header bar*\n\nWhen working with issues and pull requests, you can also\n\n* Publish to issue comment. Copilot Workspace automatically generates a comment with a share link for the session, which is included in the issue. This allows reviewers to quickly access the workspace session and see the proposed changes.\n\n* Publish to pull request comment. Similar to the issue comment, Copilot Workspace automatically generates a comment with a share link for the session, which is included in the pull request. This allows reviewers to quickly access the workspace session and see the proposed changes.\n\n## Task Completion\n\nWhen a task is implemented, validated, and reviewed, you can complete the task in different ways, depending on the type of task you’re working on.\n\n<img src=\"images/overview/task-completion.png\" width=600 alt=\"Creating a pull request\">\n\n*Creating a pull request for the implemented changes*\n\n| Task type | Available completions | \n|-----------| -------------------- |\n| Issue | — Create pull request <br> — Create draft pull request <br> — Push to new branch <br> — Push changes to current branch (only available if you have commit permissions to the repo) <br> <br> These may fork the repository if you do not have write access |\n| Ad-hoc task | — *As for issues* |\n| PR task | — Update pull request (pushes a new commit with the changes) <br> — *As for issues* |\n| Repo task | — Create repository (creates a new repo from the selected template repo, and includes the changes) |\n\n## Session Dashboard\n\nCopilot Workspace automatically saves your work. It also provides a session dashboard, which allows you to easily resume your work later. You can start a task from your phone and then finish up on your laptop, or vice versa.\n\n<img src=\"images/general/dashboard.png\" width=600 alt=\"Copilot Workspace dashboard\">\n\n*The Copilot Workspace dashboard showing recent, bookmarked and completed sessions*\n\nUndo and redo are supported within the session via buttons on the toolbar.\n\n## Appendix: Glossary\n\n| Term | Definition |\n|------|------------|\n| Copilot Workspace | A Copilot-native dev environment that’s designed for exploring and completing every day tasks  |\n| Target | A branch of a codebase at a specific commit | \n| Task | A natural language description of a change to a target | \n| Topic | A brief single-sentence summary of a task, usually in question form |\n| Specification | A description of the current and proposed state of the target as it relates to the task |\n| Plan | A list of files to add, remove or change, with notes about each of them, that together transform the target from its current state to its proposed state |\n| Implementation | A set of changes to the target that, when applied, will complete the task |\n| Session | A user’s saved progress towards completing a task, a single task can have many sessions |\n| Snapshot session | A snapshot of a user’s session, created when you click “Share link”, including both the task progress and UX state |","is_error":false}]},"uuid":"4c5a0fb7-5951-49f8-903f-593e38d7f348","timestamp":"2026-03-20T13:40:23.395Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"7fc113f9-b746-4420-80ba-3cfd9b259695","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/Users/mohamedabdallah/Work/Wazir","sessionId":"0c5a9bfa-6816-42b1-a97d-37ae7dad26c5","version":"2.1.80","gitBranch":"feat/remaining-6-fixes","slug":"mighty-wondering-giraffe"}

{"parentUuid":"420e69b8-c9c4-4df2-acad-c4dcecad9c0b","isSidechain":true,"promptId":"993bfee3-85a0-4b7f-b82a-ba53f3d6daa1","agentId":"aab6decea538aac41","type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_012WbNknf8fT6Jitn1crbnHN","type":"tool_result","content":"# ideation\n\nMulti-agent ideation skill for [Claude Code](https://claude.com/claude-code). Launches a team of specialized agents that explore a concept through structured dialogue between a Free Thinker and a Grounder, arbitrated by the team lead, and documented by a Writer. Three actions control the workflow: **Plan** (pre-flight interview), **Ideate** (depth-aware exploration + conditional production), and **Continue** (versioned session resumption).\n\n## Agents\n\n| Agent | Role |\n|-------|------|\n| **Arbiter** | Team lead — coordinates, evaluates idea reports, signals convergence |\n| **Free Thinker** | Divergent generation — creative leaps, unexpected connections, \"what if...\" |\n| **Grounder** | Convergent editing — winnows ideas, keeps the brainstorm on brief |\n| **Writer** | Synthesis and memory — observes dialogue, produces briefs and the vision document |\n| **Explorer** | Research — investigates background topics, existing solutions, citations (spawned on demand) |\n| **Image Agent** | Creates infographic visuals for each idea via ChatGPT image generation |\n| **Presentation Agent** | Builds a PowerPoint deck from the vision document |\n| **Web Page Agent** | Designs a polished, self-contained HTML distribution page |\n| **Archivist** | Produces a Results PDF and a comprehensive Session Capsule PDF |\n\n## Install\n\nClone this repo into your `.claude/skills/` directory — either **project-level** (just this project) or **personal/global** (all projects):\n\n**Project-level** (committed with your repo, available to collaborators):\n```bash\nmkdir -p .claude/skills\ngit clone https://github.com/bladnman/ideation .claude/skills/ideation\n```\n\n**Personal/global** (available in all your Claude Code sessions):\n```bash\nmkdir -p ~/.claude/skills\ngit clone https://github.com/bladnman/ideation ~/.claude/skills/ideation\n```\n\nClaude Code discovers skills automatically — no restart needed. Verify it's installed by typing `/ideation` in a Claude Code session.\n\n### Where things go\n\n```\n.claude/skills/ideation/       # or ~/.claude/skills/ideation/\n├── SKILL.md                   # Skill definition (required)\n├── README.md\n└── templates/                 # Supporting files referenced by SKILL.md\n    ├── idea-brief.md\n    ├── idea-report.md\n    ├── ideation-graph.md\n    ├── lineage.md             # Version chain tracking for continuations\n    ├── prd.md\n    ├── session-config.yaml    # Session configuration schema\n    ├── session-summary.md\n    └── vision-document.md\n```\n\n### Updating\n\n```bash\ncd .claude/skills/ideation && git pull    # project-level\ncd ~/.claude/skills/ideation && git pull   # personal/global\n```\n\n## Requirements\n\n- Claude Code with [Agent Teams](https://docs.anthropic.com/en/docs/claude-code) enabled:\n  ```bash\n  claude config set env.CLAUDE_CODE_EXPERIMENTAL_AGENT_TEAMS 1\n  ```\n- Opus 4.6 model (agent teams require it)\n- `python-pptx` and `weasyprint` for production artifacts (installed automatically during the session)\n- Chrome with the Claude-in-Chrome extension (for image generation via ChatGPT)\n\n## Usage\n\n### New session (Plan → Ideate)\n\nEvery new session starts with the **Plan** action — a brief interview to configure depth, outputs, and research before the team is spawned.\n\n```\n/ideation path/to/concept-seed.md\n```\n\nOr with an inline description:\n\n```\n/ideation \"An app that turns voice memos into structured project briefs\"\n```\n\nOr with no argument (the interview will ask for the concept):\n\n```\n/ideation\n```\n\nThe Plan action will ask you:\n1. **Depth** — how deep should exploration go?\n   - **Quick** (~15-30 min): 2-3 directions, fast convergence\n   - **Standard** (~45-90 min): 3-5 directions, balanced exploration *(default)*\n   - **Deep** (~2-3 hrs): 5-8 directions, thorough exploration\n   - **Exhaustive** (~3+ hrs): 8+ directions, comprehensive mapping\n2. **Outputs** — which deliverables to produce (all, subset, or session artifacts only)\n3. *(If needed)* Domain clarification or research confirmation\n\nAfter confirmation, the Ideate action launches the team with your configured settings.\n\n### Continue a previous session\n\nResume and build on a previous ideation session. Creates a **new versioned directory** — the parent session is never modified.\n\n```\n/ideation continue ideation-distributed-systems-20260219-143052/\n```\n\nOr search by keyword (matches against `ideation-*<keyword>*` directories in CWD):\n\n```\n/ideation continue distributed-systems\n```\n\nContinue mode:\n- **Discovers** matching sessions with summaries (date, depth, brief count)\n- **Creates a versioned directory** (e.g., `ideation-voice-memos-v2-20260221-091500/`)\n- **Runs a mini-interview** asking what to focus on, depth, and outputs\n- **References parent materials** (vision doc, briefs, ideation graph) so the team builds on prior work\n- **Tracks lineage** in `session/LINEAGE.md`\n\nVersioning scheme:\n```\nOriginal:     ideation-voice-memos-20260219-143052/\nContinue:     ideation-voice-memos-v2-20260221-091500/\nContinue:     ideation-voice-memos-v3-20260222-140000/\nBranch:       ideation-voice-memos-v2a-20260222-150000/\n```\n\n### Generate a PRD from a session\n\nAfter an ideation session completes, generate a Product Requirements Document:\n\n```\n/ideation prd ideation-distributed-systems-20260219-143052/\n```\n\nOr search by keyword:\n\n```\n/ideation prd distributed-systems\n```\n\nPRD mode is a solo operation — no team is spawned. It reads the session's vision document, briefs, and other artifacts, then produces a `PRD_<concept-slug>.md` that focuses on *what* to build and *why*, preserving the ideation session's intent, emotional logic, and language.\n\n## How It Works\n\nThe system separates cognitive modes across distinct roles because combining them in a single agent produces biased output:\n\n- **Generation** should not evaluate its own work\n- **Evaluation** should not try to also create\n- **Synthesis** should have no perspective to protect\n- **Research** should report facts, not generate ideas\n\nA human provides the seed concept. The **Plan** action interviews the user to configure depth and outputs. The **Ideate** action spawns the team: two dialogue agents (Free Thinker + Grounder) explore the space, the Arbiter evaluates idea reports and steers toward convergence using depth-aware rules, and the Writer observes and synthesizes into a vision document. When ideation converges, production agents (conditionally spawned based on output selection) transform the output into distributable artifacts.\n\n### Depth levels\n\n| | Quick | Standard | Deep | Exhaustive |\n|---|---|---|---|---|\n| Directions explored | 2-3 | 3-5 | 5-8 | 8+ |\n| Time estimate | ~15-30 min | ~45-90 min | ~2-3 hrs | ~3+ hrs |\n| Convergence | Fast | Balanced | Thorough | Comprehensive |\n| Best for | Well-defined concepts, time pressure | General exploration | Complex/ambiguous domains | Foundational concepts |\n\n### Output tiers\n\n**Tier 1** (always produced): Vision doc, briefs, session summary, ideation graph, snapshots, idea reports.\n\n**Tier 2** (user-selectable): Distribution page (HTML), Results PDF, Capsule PDF, PPTX presentation, infographic images. Production agents are only spawned for selected outputs.\n\n**Minimal path**: If no Tier 2 outputs are selected, the production phase is skipped entirely.\n\n## Output\n\nEach invocation creates a uniquely named directory inside an `ideations/`\nparent folder, keeping all sessions organized:\n\n```\nideations/\n  ideation-<slug>-<YYYYMMDD-HHMMSS>/\n    index.html                       # Designed distribution page (if selected)\n    RESULTS_<concept>.pdf            # PDF of the distribution page (if selected)\n    CAPSULE_<concept>.pdf            # Comprehensive session archive (if selected)\n    PRESENTATION_<concept>.pptx      # Slide deck (if selected)\n    PRD_<concept>.md                 # Product requirements (generated via /ideation prd)\n    images/                          # Infographic images (if selected)\n\n    session/\n      session-config.yaml            # Session configuration\n      VISION_<concept>.md            # Consolidated vision document\n      SESSION_SUMMARY.md             # Session summary\n      ideation-graph.md              # Living graph of the dialogue\n      LINEAGE.md                     # Version chain (for continuations)\n      sources/                       # All original input materials\n      research/                      # Explorer's research reports\n      briefs/                        # Final idea briefs\n      idea-reports/                  # Raw idea reports from dialogue\n      snapshots/                     # Writer's version snapshots\n\n    build/\n      build_capsule.py               # Regenerates both PDFs\n      build_presentation.py          # Regenerates the PPTX\n```\n\nExample: `ideations/ideation-voice-memos-20260219-143052/`\n\n## License\n\nMIT","is_error":false}]},"uuid":"20a30229-f083-4e35-a064-b1d28f815296","timestamp":"2026-03-20T13:40:25.452Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"420e69b8-c9c4-4df2-acad-c4dcecad9c0b","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/Users/mohamedabdallah/Work/Wazir","sessionId":"0c5a9bfa-6816-42b1-a97d-37ae7dad26c5","version":"2.1.80","gitBranch":"feat/remaining-6-fixes","slug":"mighty-wondering-giraffe"}

Here's a summary of the key concepts and code patterns:\n\n## Command Object Overview\n\nThe Command object is a versatile primitive for controlling graph execution that accepts four parameters: update (to apply state updates), goto (to navigate to specific nodes), graph (to target a parent graph when navigating from subgraphs), and resume (to provide a value to resume execution after an interrupt).\n\n## Basic Pattern: Combining State Updates and Control Flow\n\nUse update to modify state (e.g., saving customer information looked up during a conversation) and goto to route to a specific node after the tool completes.\n\nHere's a basic code pattern example:\n\nA node can return a Command object with both state update and routing: `return new Command({ update: { foo: \"a\", }, goto: nodeB or nodeC })`.\n\n## Resume Pattern for Interrupts\n\nWhen using interrupts, the pattern involves pausing the graph at an interrupt point and then resuming with a value: `result = graph.invoke(Command(resume=\"yes\"), config)`.\n\n## Advanced Pattern: Dynamic Navigation with State Updates\n\nA common pattern is to use Command with conditional logic to both update state and route: `return Command(update={\"assessment_status\": \"success\"}, goto=\"Output Requirement Analysis\")`.\n\n## Known Issue with Update State During Interruption\n\nWhen updating state while in an interrupted state using Command with goto and update, the graph.get_state().next may return empty because Command(goto=) returns the next state dynamically which update_state may not be handling properly.\n\nThe search results provide practical examples and documentation from LangChain's official sources covering these patterns for 2024-2025 LangGraph versions.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown when you select an action it will immediately take effect.\n\n<img src=\"https://github.com/user-attachments/assets/95228cfd-3031-49b8-8e0f-d47b5c98d4ae\" width=\"400\" >\n\n#### Consolidating the Plan Action Buttons\n\nWe have also consolidated plan action buttons like Regenerate and Add File to a kebab menu.\n\n**Before:**  \n<img src=\"https://github.com/user-attachments/assets/d049b613-b48a-4494-80eb-a4a92f1c00dd\" width=\"400\" >\n\n**After:**  \n<img src=\"https://github.com/user-attachments/assets/2f5a89f6-00c7-42cc-81aa-381f928cb34c\" width=\"400\" >\n\n### VS Code Extension Updates\n\n- **Enhanced Session List:** Sessions now appear earlier in their lifecycle in the session list, supporting the new brainstorming feature in VS Code.\n- **Stale View Fix:** Resolved an issue where stale view states were retained in certain views.\n- **Push to Branch / PR Creation Fix:** Fixed failures when merging into an existing branch with updates to the same files.\n- **Binary Detection Fix:** Addressed a false positive issue where folders were incorrectly flagged as binary after session syncing stopped.\n- **Error Message Visibility:** Resolved cases where certain error messages did not display.\n\n## 📅 22 November 2024\n\nComing off the back of [GitHub Universe](https://githubuniverse.com/), we have been making a ton of behind the scenes change, to improve stability, performance, and setting ourselves up for the next stage of product growth. This is a pretty significant capability, since it unlocks new ways of defining your intent, such as using...\n\n1. **Architecture/class diagrams**, that outline the structure of code you want to generate ([example](https://copilot-workspace.githubnext.com/githubnext/workspace-blank?shareId=25cc2d17-3d00-4f6c-ad5b-ccf3e53a992c))\n2. **App screenshots**, which visually highlight a part of the UI you want to change\n3. **UI mock-ups**, which describe the layout/behavior of some client code you want to write\n4. **Photographs**, which capture hand-written notes or drawings from a meeting\n\n<img src=\"https://github.com/user-attachments/assets/2020b755-b2e3-4639-9871-973cad184b64\" width=\"900px\" /><br />\n\nWhen combined with [custom instructions](#custom-instructions) and [web URLs](#external-context), AI vision expands CW's support for managing task context, and defining your tasks in whichever way is most natural.
